<div id="review_673201785" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/673201785" />
    <a title="Alan" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5650849-alan"><img alt="Alan" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1310234599p2/5650849.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/673201785?book_show_action=true">Jul 21, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Alan" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Alan" href="/user/show/5650849-alan">Alan</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer673201785" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10399584668609826212">Read this in an NEH (to which great institution, now threatened, I owe my mental growth once I was teaching) seminar on Medicine and the Humanities, at Cornell, with Psychiatry (and German) professor Sander Gilman, later President of the 15K member Modern Language Association of America. Much of our seminar became his later books like Seeing the Insane--images, representaations of patients, but also of nurses and physicians. So art history was involved, and even a bit of music, since Sander's wi</span>
  <span id="freeText10399584668609826212" style="display:none">Read this in an NEH (to which great institution, now threatened, I owe my mental growth once I was teaching) seminar on Medicine and the Humanities, at Cornell, with Psychiatry (and German) professor Sander Gilman, later President of the 15K member Modern Language Association of America. Much of our seminar became his later books like Seeing the Insane--images, representaations of patients, but also of nurses and physicians. So art history was involved, and even a bit of music, since Sander's wife was a pro. <br /> My own study was of Chronobiology--largely using not my desk at the Olin Library, but the Cornell Agricultural Library across campus, an older building that reminded me of State College Libraries. They had a great collection of three or four periodicals on chronobiology--diurnal rhythms, and monthly, or circavigintan rhythms, or annual, etc. Does Insanity have a monthly cyle, as in the colloquial "Loonies" for "lunatics," Moon-struck. This followed up on my study of 17C lunar mapping in another NEH, this one of the last for a full year, at Brown University, on 14&amp;15C Italian Popular Culture. For that seminar my undergrad prof Archibald MacLeish recommended me, and praised my resulting essay so highly I quoted it on the back of my first book on Giordano Bruno. <br /> I worked on that book for forty years, never did all 400 pp in Latin, but read most of his other 25 books in Italian and Latin. I began reading GB two months after we landed on the Moon, July 1969; I found Bruno's 400p Latin poem, De innumerabilibis, immenso universo &amp; mundis...1592 / 1880s), began the 4th Ch, On Copernicus. Couldn't believe how spot-on he was with a satellite-like circling of the Earth and flight to the Moon. Only thing off, the Moon was inhabited--as he believed there were habitable worlds around every star, an infitnite number: the Inquisitors insisted he give up his mistaken belief, which Harvard Center for Astrophysics is now researching. (Google my talk there,"Giordano Bruno Harvard Video.")<br /> Forgive me if I cannot recall exactly how Nana, and especially Manet's Bar at the Folies Bergeres, fit into the course, but I do recall discussions of how nurses and physicians were represented in films and on TV. And discussions of sexuality and the "other," and sometimes not the other, but oneself: I composed, with my fellow community-college teacher Gus Pelletier, "Onania! Onania! God shed his grace on thee..."</span>
  <a data-text-id="10399584668609826212" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/132929396" />
    <a title="Andrew" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/442654-andrew"><img alt="Andrew" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1513093267p2/442654.jpg" /></a>

<span id="freeTextContainer7118818788693850029">This is Nana. Watch Nana fuck. Fuck, Nana, fuck.<br/><br/>That is the plot of Emile Zola's Nana. It is a 19th Century French novel, which means it's this big messy melodramatic soap opera. But it's so much fun! Nana is a man-eater to make anyone on Days of Our Lives blush, tangled up not only in prostitution, but in gambling, gluttony, promiscuity, lesbian kidnappings (?!), sadomasochism, suicide, murder, and, most importantly for Zola, economic catastrophe. Not only can she burn down the lives of those a</span>
  <span id="freeText7118818788693850029" style="display:none">This is Nana. Watch Nana fuck. Fuck, Nana, fuck.<br /><br />That is the plot of Emile Zola's Nana. It is a 19th Century French novel, which means it's this big messy melodramatic soap opera. But it's so much fun! Nana is a man-eater to make anyone on Days of Our Lives blush, tangled up not only in prostitution, but in gambling, gluttony, promiscuity, lesbian kidnappings (?!), sadomasochism, suicide, murder, and, most importantly for Zola, economic catastrophe. Not only can she burn down the lives of those around her, she can also destroy whole civilizations. She's a symbol for decay in Deuxième-Empire France, which means that in the end she like actually decays. And it's grislier than a Cronenberg movie. Wooo!!!!</span>
  <a data-text-id="7118818788693850029" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<span id="freeTextContainer2302882687690569797">I couldn't help but laugh. It just seems funny and crazy in a lampooning way. It's full of sex and the stupidity of people. The more it went on, the more I hoped that the vapid and well-meaning but fiscally and sexually voracious prostitute Nana of the title would screw everyone over, including herself. As she did, repeatedly. It's such a French romp. I mean, really. France produces <i>120 Days of Sodom</i>, <i>Les Liaisons Dangereuses</i>, <i>The Story of O</i>.. and <i>Nana</i>. <br/><br/><blockquote>
  <i>"Nana shot through like a cloud of inva</i></blockquote></span>
  <span id="freeText2302882687690569797" style="display:none">I couldn't help but laugh. It just seems funny and crazy in a lampooning way. It's full of sex and the stupidity of people. The more it went on, the more I hoped that the vapid and well-meaning but fiscally and sexually voracious prostitute Nana of the title would screw everyone over, including herself. As she did, repeatedly. It's such a French romp. I mean, really. France produces <i>120 Days of Sodom</i>, <i>Les Liaisons Dangereuses</i>, <i>The Story of O</i>.. and <i>Nana</i>. <br /><br /><blockquote>
  <i>"Nana shot through like a cloud of invading locusts, a devastating fire flattening a whole province. Wherever she set her little foot, there was burnt earth. Farm by farm, meadow after meadow, she gobbled up his inheritance without even noticing, just as she gobbled up a bag of sugared almonds on her lap in between meals."</i>
</blockquote><br />Full of characters, the most memorable were Georges, a boy in love with Nana who Nana has a fancy for and who ends up stabbing himself with her scissors, Satin a street prostitute that Nana has an on-off homosexual affair with, and Count Muffat, the cuckolded aristocrat hopelessly infatuated with her and who endures almost anything at her hands. Domestic violence, the theatre and gambling appear here too in this rushing novel about the rises and falls of an evidently charismatic but inexorably human prostitute. <br /><br /></span>

<div id="review_291097056" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/291097056" />

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer291097056" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9922582178820752779">Nana is the daughter of Gervaise from <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/92967.L_Assommoir__The_Dram_Shop___Les_Rougon_Macquart___7_' title='L'Assommoir (The Dram Shop) (Les Rougon-Macquart, #7) by Émile Zola' rel='nofollow noopener'>L'Assommoir (The Dram Shop)</a>. Nana is a prostitute, hedonist, and narcissist. She has enormous sex appeal, able to attract men of enormous wealth with the crook of her finger.<br/><br/>It was very interesting reading this practically on the heels of Balzac's <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/59144.Cousin_Bette_' title='Cousin Bette  by Honoré de Balzac' rel='nofollow noopener'>Cousin Bette</a>, which had a similar theme. Balzac is told more from the view of the men, while Zola told from the female viewpoint. Nana's character is very well-developed - one is both fascinated and repelled. The p</span>
  <span id="freeText9922582178820752779" style="display:none">Nana is the daughter of Gervaise from <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/92967.L_Assommoir__The_Dram_Shop___Les_Rougon_Macquart___7_" title="L'Assommoir (The Dram Shop) (Les Rougon-Macquart, #7) by Émile Zola" rel="nofollow noopener">L'Assommoir (The Dram Shop)</a>. Nana is a prostitute, hedonist, and narcissist. She has enormous sex appeal, able to attract men of enormous wealth with the crook of her finger.<br /><br />It was very interesting reading this practically on the heels of Balzac's <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/59144.Cousin_Bette_" title="Cousin Bette  by Honoré de Balzac" rel="nofollow noopener">Cousin Bette</a>, which had a similar theme. Balzac is told more from the view of the men, while Zola told from the female viewpoint. Nana's character is very well-developed - one is both fascinated and repelled. The primary male, Count Muffat, is also well-developed, though perhaps just slightly less so.<br /><br />For those who might not be so attracted to a story of a prostitute, included in this story is a fascinating and detailed description of mid-19th Century theatre. The opening and third chapters take place in the Variete with its atmosphere of anticipation on opening night, the stage construction itself, and a good sense of backstage and dressing rooms.<br /><br /></span>

<div id="review_172632829" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/172632829" />
    <a title="Alex" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5522229-alex"><img alt="Alex" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1318921454p2/5522229.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/172632829?book_show_action=true">May 31, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Alex" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Alex" href="/user/show/5522229-alex">Alex</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5522229-alex?shelf=literature">literature</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5522229-alex?shelf=french-original">french-original</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer172632829" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5067195353162303688">For those who like time travel, not to a virtual time someone makes up, but to a real one such as Paris in the 1870’s, those who want vivid, detailed, and realistic imagery, minimal moralization, or those who want to sample “naturalist” or “scientific” literature, Nana is a perfect specimen. <br/><br/>Here we follow a first-rate Parisian courtesan into her home, every room, including the bathroom, to see her clients, what they do, talk about, eat, and how much money changes hands, what her room maid, cook</span>
  <span id="freeText5067195353162303688" style="display:none">For those who like time travel, not to a virtual time someone makes up, but to a real one such as Paris in the 1870’s, those who want vivid, detailed, and realistic imagery, minimal moralization, or those who want to sample “naturalist” or “scientific” literature, Nana is a perfect specimen. <br /><br />Here we follow a first-rate Parisian courtesan into her home, every room, including the bathroom, to see her clients, what they do, talk about, eat, and how much money changes hands, what her room maid, cook, coachman, butler, and guard do and gossip about, how they steal money in their each one’s own way, how her worshipers and collecting creditors behave while waiting there, etc. The details of most furniture, fabric, flowers, and many decorative items are described down to shape, material, color, etc. There is a huge difference in these between her early apartment (7 rooms, 1 servant) before her huge success and her mansion (5 servants) after that. I learn for the 1st time that an upholstery fabric can be specifically weaved, not printed or cut, to a design to fit the intended chair. <br /><br />Someone might doubt whether these descriptions are faithful to reality. According to the preface to the 1st English edition by Henry James, Zola had researched every scene in the novel, with one separate book of notes for each of them, plus one notebook devoted to the physiology of each of the personages. He got into some apartment or house typical to such courtesan and spent much time recording just about everything there. (He also wanted to know the detailed appearance of a corpse died of smallpox but was not allowed to go and see, so he sent someone to record for him.)<br /><br />Here is a list of dishes and drinks at a dinner party in Nana’s apartment (sorry for my notes included in the parentheses):<br />- du Meursault (a wine)<br />- Purée d'asperges comtesse, consommé à la Deslignac (Countess’ asparagus thick soup, La Deslignac clear soup);<br />- crépinettes de lapereaux aux truffes et des niokys au parmesan (Truffled flat sausage of young rabbit meat (in casing of sheep abdominal membrane), and Niokys (?) with Parmesan cheese);<br />- carpe du Rhin à la Chambord et une selle de chevreuil à l'anglaise (Chambord Rhine carp, and an English style saddle of venison);<br />- des poulardes à la maréchale, des filets de sole sauce ravigote et des escalopes de foie gras (Mrs. Marshall’s fattened chicken, sole fillet in shallot spicy sauce, and foie gras cutlet);<br />- Chambertinet du Léoville (a wine)<br />- sorbets aux mandarines (mandarin sorbet)<br />- Le rôti chaud… filet aux truffes (hot roast -- truffled beef fillet)<br />- le rôti froid, une galantine de pintade à la gelée (cold roast -- galantine of guineafowl &amp; jelly)<br />- des cèpes à l'italienne et des croustades d'ananas Pompadour (Italian cep mushroom, and Pompadour pineapple in pastry crust or pineapple fritter (?))<br />- champagne (throughout all the courses)<br />- les glaces et le dessert (ices and deserts)<br />- le café <br /><br />And then beyond her apartment or house we can see other people or scenes. Almost all the streets mentioned (about 20+ as I estimate?) in the novel are still there in Paris and can be searched in Google Earth. A theater and a hospital are still there, too. Most restaurants have since closed, though. In the novel there are streetwalkers, who behave very differently in the evenings, when they try to attract customers, or to escape the police, than in the mornings, when they come out to shop at the market, or in the afternoons, when they go to a cheap eatery mostly serving them.<br /><br />Nana has another career as a burlesque singer/actress. To give us several scenes in such theater, Zola researched very intensively so that we can tour everywhere in it, including the boxes, the stalls, the backstage, and even the drink bar for the actors below the stairs, etc., he even tells us the shape and height of the windows in the staircase. Running such business seems not very easy, as they don’t have enough money to renovate the hall with cracked golden moldings or some stained backstage wall coverings, and they buy props from cheap flea markets, even though at times the theater is packed with spectators, including aristocrats and even foreign princes. There are also scenes of horse racing in Paris and chateaus near Orleans, all very detailed, down to, say, the rough layout of the garden of the chateau Nana occupies for some days.<br /><br />Zola learned trade jargon of theater and uses it to describe the performance and rehearsal situations. The book is full of slang to suit the lowly characters, especially Nana; this perhaps makes different translations vary quite a lot. For example, she says “…si ta femme claquait…"; one (F. J. Vizetelly) translated it as "if your wife was to croak", but another (anonymous at gutenberg.org) did as "if your wife were only to kick the bucket".<br /><br />Zola does not avoid texts deemed as obscene at the time, such as descriptions of Nana’s body, or the phrase “to sleep with”. Translators such as Vizetelly did some censorship (boo!), changing meanings, or mostly deleting full sentences or larger passages, such as this one lost by him:<br /><br />"Nana …,ramassant des filles au coin des bornes. Quand elle rentrait en voiture, elle s'amourachait parfois d'un souillon aperçu sur le pavé, les sens pris, l'imagination lâchée; et elle faisait monter le souillon, le payait et le renvoyait. Puis, sous un déguisement d'homme, c'étaient des parties dans des maisons infâmes, des spectacles de débauche dont elle amusait son ennui.”<br /><br />Hmm, I have probably done more than I should in this review by now... <br /><br />But I almost forgot another important dimension of Zola's virtuosity! There is complexity in Nana: she is not only a voracious exterminator; she is moved to tears for the poor and donates her only money to charity; she is a slave for her true love, a mean man; she despises all, but loves, respects, and fears her childhood friend, a poor prostitute; she is sometimes honest but sometimes a liar... the contradictions make her very human.<br /><br />In summary, if you prefer the most honest or faithful, and the least manipulating, novelists or translators, as I do, then Zola, and hence Nana, are probably among the bests you can get.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="5067195353162303688" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3329226840" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3329226840" />
    <a title="Elizabeth (Alaska)" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2026178-elizabeth-alaska"><img alt="Elizabeth (Alaska)" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1304966095p2/2026178.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3329226840?book_show_action=true">Oct 31, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Elizabeth (Alaska)" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Elizabeth (Alaska)" href="/user/show/2026178-elizabeth-alaska">Elizabeth (Alaska)</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/53363401-nana">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2026178-elizabeth-alaska?shelf=author-zola">author-zola</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2026178-elizabeth-alaska?shelf=kindle">kindle</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3329226840" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4003228427782723122">Was Nana the ordinary prostitute? Not on your life! Men flocked to Nana like moths to a flame. They spent money like water and she didn't mind leaving them financially ruined and as long as they had money to spend on her she was willing to give them time and her body. This is most assuredly not graphic nor erotic, but there can be no question about what she is. <br/><br/>This was a re-read for me in an entirely different translation. I see that I gave my <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/291097056' rel='nofollow noopener'>first read</a> 4-stars. I note that because in the intr</span>
  <span id="freeText4003228427782723122" style="display:none">Was Nana the ordinary prostitute? Not on your life! Men flocked to Nana like moths to a flame. They spent money like water and she didn't mind leaving them financially ruined and as long as they had money to spend on her she was willing to give them time and her body. This is most assuredly not graphic nor erotic, but there can be no question about what she is. <br /><br />This was a re-read for me in an entirely different translation. I see that I gave my <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/291097056" rel="nofollow noopener">first read</a> 4-stars. I note that because in the introduction to this edition is said that <i>Nana</i> is Zola's 2nd best seller, losing out only to his <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/28419.La_D_b_cle" title="La Débâcle by Émile Zola" rel="nofollow noopener">La Débâcle</a>. This time around, there were really only 2 parts that were still of any value: the many scenes of the Varieté with it's rabbit warren of dressing rooms and other back stage goings on; the spectators at a significant horse racing event where some were in the grandstands but others, like Nana, attended from their private vehicles.<br /><br />Nana is surely one of the top ten most reprehensible characters in fiction. In the end, I was thankful that the debauchery and corruption and general ickiness was over. I cannot find it in my heart to give this more than 2-stars this time around.</span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9783518393994" href="/book/show/9481278-nana">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer78197429" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer503266596731765081">I don't know if I gave this book a fair shake because it was so annoying I had to stop reading it after 50 pages or so. All the women were described as "sluts" and "whores". All the men were drooling bores. And the author's tone seemed to be one of a madly gesticulating Frenchman flippantly dismissing various sexual escapades as if to say, "ah yes, sex is so boring. but what else is there to talk about?" </span>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('asRkrf+fXqmWeZ/8Op1aPQvlKBeLg6lvtwo3FcJUsLKriQEEcIoPya8hbzfZSwkYnFrRiTK/2U2ppSUuxYwKFg==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('AO+K3l4nnGSjUrpqyQmzlyiH4Fjj3KleE3WK4LK+2BfBou930TLNBJoKSqEq3+CyvzgZxlrg2XwN2pjbtWZisw==')})
  }

      var newTip = new Tip($('bookCover_780581'), "\n\n  <h2><a class=\"readable bookTitle\" href=\"https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/780581.Bel_Ami?from_choice=false&amp;from_home_module=false\">Bel-Ami<\/a><\/h2>\n\n      <div>\n        by <a class=\"authorName\" href=\"/author/show/18791.Guy_de_Maupassant?a=9&amp;origin=related_works\">Guy de Maupassant<\/a>\n      <\/div>\n\n          <div class=\"smallText uitext darkGreyText\">\n            <span class=\"minirating\"><span class=\"stars staticStars notranslate\"><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p6\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p0\"><\/span><\/span> 3.85 avg rating &mdash; 29,545 ratings<\/span>            &mdash; published 1885\n          <\/div>\n\n    <div class=\"addBookTipDescription\">\n      \n<span id=\"freeTextContainer6969457779056141526\">Guy de Maupassant\'s scandalous tale of an opportunistic young man corrupted by the allure of power, \"Bel-Ami\" is translated with an introduction by Douglas Parmee in \"Penguin Classics\". Young, attractive and very ambitious, George Duroy, known to his<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText6969457779056141526\" style=\"display:none\">Guy de Maupassant\'s scandalous tale of an opportunistic young man corrupted by the allure of power, &quot;Bel-Ami&quot; is translated with an introduction by Douglas Parmee in &quot;Penguin Classics&quot;. Young, attractive and very ambitious, George Duroy, known to his admirers as Bel-Ami, is offered a job as a journalist on La Vie francaise and soon makes a great success of his new career. But he also comes face to face with the realities of the corrupt society in which he lives - the sleazy colleagues, the manipulative mistresses, and wily financiers - and swiftly learns to become an arch-seducer, blackmailer and social climber in a world where love is only a means to an end. Written when Maupassant was at the height of his powers, &quot;Bel-Ami&quot; is a novel of great frankness and cynicism, but it is also infused with the sheer joy of life - depicting the scenes and characters of Paris in the belle epoque with wit, sensitivity, and humanity. Douglas Parmee\'s translation captures all the vigour and vitality of Maupassant\'s novel. His introduction explores the similarities between Bel-Ami and Maupassant himself and demonstrates the skill with which the author depicts his large cast of characters and the French society of the Third Republic.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"6969457779056141526\" href=\"#\" onclick=\"swapContent(\$(this));; return false;\">...more<\/a>\n\n    <\/div>\n\n      <div class=\'wtrButtonContainer An ardent devourer of sentimental novels, she longs for passion and seeks escape in fantasies of high romance, in voracious spending and, eventually, in adultery. But even her affairs bring her disappointment, and when real life continues to fail to live up to her romantic expectations, the consequences are devastating. Flaubert\'s erotically charged and psychologically acute portrayal of Emma Bovary caused a moral outcry on its publication in 1857. It was deemed so lifelike that many women claimed they were the model for his heroine; but Flaubert insisted: \'Madame Bovary, c\'est moi.\'\n\nThis modern translation by Flaubert\'s biographer, Geoffrey Wall, retains all the delicacy and precision of the French original.       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/4750._mile_Zola">About Émile Zola</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/4750._mile_Zola"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1544927603p3/4750.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/4750._mile_Zola">Émile Zola
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>
3,110
followers
</div>

</div>
</div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer11282173998668471495">Émile François Zola was an influential French novelist, the most important example of the literary school of naturalism, and a major figure in the political liberalization of France.<br/><br/>More than half of Zola's novels were part of a set of 20 books collectively known as Les Rougon-Macquart. Unlike Balzac who in the midst of his literary career resynthesized his work into La Comédie Humaine, Zola from </span>
  <span id="freeText11282173998668471495" style="display:none">Émile François Zola was an influential French novelist, the most important example of the literary school of naturalism, and a major figure in the political liberalization of France.<br /><br />More than half of Zola's novels were part of a set of 20 books collectively known as Les Rougon-Macquart. Unlike Balzac who in the midst of his literary career resynthesized his work into La Comédie Humaine, Zola from the start at the age of 28 had thought of the complete layout of the series. Set in France's Second Empire, the series traces the "environmental" influences of violence, alcohol and prostitution which became more prevalent during the second wave of the Industrial Revolution. The series examines two branches of a family: the respectable (that is, legitimate) Rougons and the disreputable (illegitimate) Macquarts for five generations.<br /><br />As he described his plans for the series, "I want to portray, at the outset of a century of liberty and truth, a family that cannot restrain itself in its rush to possess all the good things that progress is making available and is derailed by its own momentum, the fatal convulsions that accompany the birth of a new world."<br /><br />Although Zola and Cézanne were friends from childhood, they broke in later life over Zola's fictionalized depiction of Cézanne and the Bohemian life of painters in his novel <i>L'Œuvre</i> (<i>The Masterpiece</i>, 1886).<br /><br />From 1877 with the publication of <i>L'Assommoir</i>, Émile Zola became wealthy, he was better paid than Victor Hugo, for example. He became a figurehead among the literary bourgeoisie and organized cultural dinners with Guy de Maupassant, Joris-Karl Huysmans and other writers at his luxurious villa in Medan near Paris after 1880. <i>Germinal</i> in 1885, then the three 'cities', <i>Lourdes</i> in 1894, <i>Rome</i> in 1896 and <i>Paris</i> in 1897, established Zola as a successful author.<br /><br />The self-proclaimed leader of French naturalism, Zola's works inspired operas such as those of Gustave Charpentier, notably <i>Louise</i> in the 1890s. His works, inspired by the concepts of heredity (Claude Bernard), social manichaeism and idealistic socialism, resonate with those of Nadar, Manet and subsequently Flaubert.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11282173998668471495" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>

Zola ne pardonne pas ces fortunes rapides qui inondent les allées du Bois d’attelages élégants, de toilettes de Worms et de bijoux éclatants. Aristide Saccard a réussi. Living with his brother\'s family in the newly rebuilt Les Halles market, Florent is soon caught up in a dangerous maelstrom of food and politics. Amid intrigue among the market\'s sellers—the fishmonger, the charcutiere, the fruit girl, and the cheese vendor—and the glorious culinary bounty of their labors, we see the dramatic difference
